  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_949f0976-fee9-415d-931a-04b72b8560c8'
      });
    }

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer1082335928085727267">A 20-year-old Chinese painter named Stephen is sent to his family's summer home in a Japanese coastal village to recover from a bout with tuberculosis. Here he is cared for by Matsu, a reticent housekeeper and a master gardener. Over the course of a remarkable year, Stephen learns Matsu's secret and gains not only physical strength, but also profound spiritual insight. Mat</span>
  <span id="freeText1082335928085727267" style="display:none">A 20-year-old Chinese painter named Stephen is sent to his family's summer home in a Japanese coastal village to recover from a bout with tuberculosis. Here he is cared for by Matsu, a reticent housekeeper and a master gardener. Over the course of a remarkable year, Stephen learns Matsu's secret and gains not only physical strength, but also profound spiritual insight. Matsu is a samurai of the soul, a man devoted to doing good and finding beauty in a cruel and arbitrary world, and Stephen is a noble student, learning to appreciate Matsu's generous and nurturing way of life and to love Matsu's soul-mate, gentle Sachi, a woman afflicted with leprosy. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="1082335928085727267" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div class='communityQuestion'>
<div class='questionText'>
<a href="/questions/1020506-does-stephen-speak-japanese-or-does-matsu">Does Stephen speak Japanese or does Matsu and the villagers speak Chinese?</a>
</div>
<div class='communityQAFooter questionFooter'>
<ul class='questionFooterActions'>
<li class='footerItem like'>
<span class='likeItContainer' id='like_container_community_question_1020506'><a id="like_count_community_question_1020506" rel="nofollow" href="/rating/voters/1020506?resource_type=CommunityQuestion"><span class="likesCount">1 like</span></a>&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;<a rel="nofollow" class="jsLike like_it" href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/new?remember=true&amp;return_url=%2Fbook%2Fshow%2F51113.The_Samurai_s_Garden">like</a></span>

</li>
<li class='footerItem communityQuestionTimestamp'>
<a href="/questions/1020506-does-stephen-speak-japanese-or-does-matsu">3 years ago</a>
</li>
<li class='footerItem communityQuestionPermalink'>
<a href="/questions/1020506-does-stephen-speak-japanese-or-does-matsu">See all 2 answers</a>
</li>
</ul>
</div>

<div class='allCommunityAnswers'>
<div class='communityAnswer'>
<div class='communityAnswerBody'>
<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/51682376-chrystina"><img alt="" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_100x100-6433b08cbe793211f55e184d39e84c4d.png" /></a>
</div>
<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/51682376-chrystina">Chrystina</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<div class='spoiler'>
This answer contains spoilers…
<span class='spoilerAnswerText'>
<a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[
  
    Although it is never specifically clarified, when Stephen first comes to the village he talks of addressing Matsu in his poor Japanese. Then when Step<a href="#" rel="nofollow noopener">…more</a>
    Although it is never specifically clarified, when Stephen first comes to the village he talks of addressing Matsu in his poor Japanese. Then when Stephen visits Tokyo with his father and the fall of Canton is announced, his father advises him to only speak Japanese from then on until they leave.<br />Most wealthy, educated, and well-traveled individuals--and Stephen and his family fall into that category--are multilingual. With the family's close associations with Japan, it would hardly be surprising that he spoke some Japanese. And total immersion, which a year in a foreign country would certainly be, is the best way to improve your language skills.<br />On the other hand it is very unlikely that the villagers spoke Chinese. This is a small fishing village with limited educational opportunities. Even if a few spoke some limited Chinese in order to communicate better with Chinese tourists, their country is at war with China and they have sent all their young men off to fight in that war. I really cannot see them using the language of their enemy even to accommodate a guest.<br />Last, Stephen listens to Matsu's radio, which was definitely in Japanese, with no difficulty in understanding. <br />For all these reasons I assumed that Stephen was speaking Japanese throughout his stay in Tasumi except during his father's visits, when of course they would have spoken Chinese.<a href="#" rel="nofollow noopener">(less)</a>
  
 <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>
</span>
</div>
</span>
</div>

<div id="review_1626320506" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1626320506" />
    <a title="Jim Fonseca" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6743601-jim-fonseca"><img alt="Jim Fonseca" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1397669173p2/6743601.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1626320506?book_show_action=true">May 01, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jim Fonseca" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jim Fonseca" href="/user/show/6743601-jim-fonseca">Jim Fonseca</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6743601-jim-fonseca?shelf=japan">japan</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1626320506" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer461099166764634587">This novel very much focuses on Japanese culture in 1930’s rural Japan. At that time Japan was invading China and savaging China’s people and cities. Yet life goes on in the rural village pretty much as usual with the exception that all the young men are missing as they are off at war.<br/><br/>(Edited 5/11/19 to hide spoilers and add photos) <br/><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1557591418i/27489833._SX540_.jpg' width='363' height='242' alt='description' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>The main character is not Japanese, but a young Chinese man from Hong Kong. His father is a wheeler-dealer businessman who does much business in Japan as his father </span>
  <span id="freeText461099166764634587" style="display:none">This novel very much focuses on Japanese culture in 1930’s rural Japan. At that time Japan was invading China and savaging China’s people and cities. Yet life goes on in the rural village pretty much as usual with the exception that all the young men are missing as they are off at war.<br><br>(Edited 5/11/19 to hide spoilers and add photos) <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1557591418i/27489833._SX540_.jpg" width="363" height="242" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>The main character is not Japanese, but a young Chinese man from Hong Kong. His father is a wheeler-dealer businessman who does much business in Japan as his father before him did. So the father inherited a house with a caretaker in this rural seafront Japanese village. <br><br>While in the village he hears of the war only from a distance -- from letters from friends and family and from occasional radio broadcast that the servant/caretaker immediately turns off when he hears the young man approaching. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[The boy contracts tuberculosis and leaves college and his family in Hong Kong to recuperate in Japan for a year. He also learns by letter from his mother of his father’s on-going affair with a Japanese woman. He has to deal with these emotions toward his mother and father – again – at a distance. And what about the caretaker --- has he known all along of the father’s affair?  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1557591251i/27489827.jpg" width="363" height="242" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>It’s a very atmospheric novel. We learn of Japanese daily life as well as festivals and celebrations, religious ceremonies, food and cooking. Much of the limited plot revolves around the caretaker’s support for a nearby leper village and a love triangle that existed between the caretaker, a woman who was (and still is) beautiful before she became a leper, and her former fiancée who abandoned her when she came down with the disease. The boy frequently visits the leper village and becomes acquainted with its inhabitants. Both the caretaker and the woman find fulfillment in their painstaking care of their gardens. <br><br>The boy also has a puppy love romance with a young Japanese woman whose father is fiercely anti-Chinese. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ In the end the boy returns home to Hong Kong enriched by what he has learned about Japanese culture but equally decimated by how the Japanese have ravaged his home country while they stand poised to take over Hong Kong.  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br><br>Top photo from pandotrip.com; bottom from gstatic.com </["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></span>
  <a data-text-id="461099166764634587" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_120963405" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/120963405" />
    <a title="Chrissie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/632247-chrissie"><img alt="Chrissie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1321799585p2/632247.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/120963405?book_show_action=true">Sep 10, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Chrissie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Chrissie" href="/user/show/632247-chrissie">Chrissie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780641961557" href="/book/show/6979827-the-samurai-s-garden">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=hf">hf</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=text-checked">text-checked</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=japan">japan</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=china">china</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer120963405" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5639466420273605224">NO SPOILERS<br/><br/>This book is beautiful. The primary theme is about beauty. (And then there is a love story too.) I think beauty is very important in the Japanese culture. What really is beauty? The tempo is lulling, quiet, and calming. This too creates a kind of beauty. The concept of beauty is drawn through the lives of the main characters. The novel takes place at the time of the Rape on Nanking, when the Japanese were invading and slaughtering the Chinese at the end of the 1930s. In contrast, the </span>
  <span id="freeText5639466420273605224" style="display:none">NO SPOILERS<br /><br />This book is beautiful. The primary theme is about beauty. (And then there is a love story too.) I think beauty is very important in the Japanese culture. What really is beauty? The tempo is lulling, quiet, and calming. This too creates a kind of beauty. The concept of beauty is drawn through the lives of the main characters. The novel takes place at the time of the Rape on Nanking, when the Japanese were invading and slaughtering the Chinese at the end of the 1930s. In contrast, the novel plays out in a small Japanese village outside of Kobe. You have the quiet village life following the seasons, the routine Japanese festivals and the pervading sense of tranguilty that gardens and gardening create. Honor and respect and traditions of non-confrontation/silence still cannot stamp out the rumbling fears and problems of the time. Not only are we confronted with hatred between Japanese and Chinese but also the fear and dishonor associated with leprosy, the lepers having been pushed out to the village Yamaguchi. Silence and honor cannot remove the threats of natural disasters, fires and storms of nature and of people. These contrasting forces play out against each other. On one side you have the lulling order of society, and yet underneath rumbles these threatening problems. All would seem less threatening if allowed to surface. <br /><br />This novel teaches the reader about Japanese traditions. The prose style fits the message. Look at Indian art. Compare it to Chinese art and then to Japanese art. You clearly see that as we move eastward all the nonessentials are removed. What is left is pure and simple. Art is incorporated into all parts of daily life - house construction, bathing, gardening, the use of scrolls, food preparation, to name but a few. This pure, simple form of art is also reflected in how the Japanese interact with each other.<br /><br />I think you will be surprised about who the main protagonists are in this novel. I do not see Stephen in this role. It is Matsu and Sachi. So be a bit patient when you begin the novel. You will delight in the life story of the main two protagonists. Remember, beauty is not always what is on the surface. I said this book was about beauty. Well it is. But What is beauty? Is it that we see only with our eyes? Is it the clicking of stones when you rake a stone garden? Is it the delight of soaking in a hot, clean bath?, Is it all of these things? Is it something more? <br /><br />P.S. How the Japanese switch between Shinto beliefs and Buddhism for different ceremonies never ceses to amaze me!<br /><br />Stephen and his family seemed a way for the author to incorporate the confict between the Japanese and the Chinese into the plot. I do not think this was adequately explored. I found it hard to believe the ease with which they were accepted by the Japanese. Maybe that is why I chose three rather than four stars. <br /><br />And was Yamaguchi truly a Japanese leper colony, or is this fictitious?!</span>
  <a data-text-id="5639466420273605224" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText11291336806226553879" style="display:none">Beautifully written and hypnotic. <div id="review_145317773" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/145317773" />
    <a title="Aubrey" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4119460-aubrey"><img alt="Aubrey" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1472535045p2/4119460.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/145317773?book_show_action=true">Feb 01, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Aubrey" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Aubrey" href="/user/show/4119460-aubrey">Aubrey</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=3-star">3-star</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=r-goodreads">r-goodreads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=person-of-everything">person-of-everything</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=antidote-think-twice-read">antidote-think-twice-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=antidote-think-twice-all">antidote-think-twice-all</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=reality-check">reality-check</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=r-2013">r-2013</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=person-of-reality">person-of-reality</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer145317773" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4161560946049912803">3.5/5<br/><br/>The jury's still out on this one, but frankly, I know myself too well to believe that they'll ever return with a definitive answer in hand.<br/><br/>Relying simply on gut instinct, I enjoyed the book. I have never traveled outside the US, and the cosmopolitan feeling of having aspects of China, Japan, and vague traces of Western culture all wrapped into one story was appealing, to say the least. The appeal for me was strengthened by the majority of the story taking place in Japanese landscapes filled</span>
  <span id="freeText4161560946049912803" style="display:none">3.5/5<br /><br />The jury's still out on this one, but frankly, I know myself too well to believe that they'll ever return with a definitive answer in hand.<br /><br />Relying simply on gut instinct, I enjoyed the book. I have never traveled outside the US, and the cosmopolitan feeling of having aspects of China, Japan, and vague traces of Western culture all wrapped into one story was appealing, to say the least. The appeal for me was strengthened by the majority of the story taking place in Japanese landscapes filled with calm and natural beauty, an aesthetic that the prose made an especial effort to convey. However, there were many things that increasingly bothered me as the pages progressed, and were never satisfactorily resolved enough for my taste.<br /><br />My biggest issue was with the main character, who as the sole first person narrator was entrusted with setting and maintaining the tone through the entirety of the novel. This tone is a very comforting, but ultimately placid one, and it is hard to believe someone would be so overwhelmingly lighthearted and good-naturedly accepting considering all the events swirling around him. Tuberculosis, being in a foreign country that is currently attacking your homeland, leprosy, suicide, parental issues, and so many other major events that seemed to only register for brief moments within the narrator's mind as a side note to an entry. However, I fully admit to being introspective in reaction to external conflicts to the point of neurotic anxiety, so I may be judging the character too harshly in terms of how he chooses to deal with all the chaos around him. <br /><br />It still seems odd, though, his ability to block out major concerns and focus on the smaller events of the much less chaotic everyday life. One event in particular makes me believe that the author used the epistolary form as a means to achieve exactly that, namely when the narrator <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[receives a letter concerning the Nanjing Massacre. Just as he sits down to compose a letter to his family and friends back on the mainland, time skips forward more than two weeks, and there is little mention of what had to have resulted in huge amounts of mental conflict <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>. Ultimately, all conflict both physical and mental is received secondhand by the narrator, and he maintains his well meaning nature all the way through without any repercussions or consequent soul-searching. Forgive me my cynicism, but golden boy characters such as these don't seem the most realistic of beings. <br /><br />There was an also worrying amount of focus on physical beauty being a synonym for moral goodness, an example being the numerous occasions when the narrator evaluated and reacted to characters seemingly for how their faces look and/or might have looked in the past. I don't see his interest in being a painter, a good looking one at that as mentioned by many of the other characters, as a good enough excuse for this constant preoccupation and lack of further insight into those around him, especially when concerning the females.<br /><br />There's also the Second Sino-Japanese War to consider, which began before the events of the book and ended long after, fully subsuming this tale of a young Chinese man and his father traveling back and forth between the two participating countries. I have very little hard knowledge of the events, so it is near impossible for me to gauge how feasible it would have been for this kind of story to have actually happened. While it is obvious that the author made an effort to include pertinent cultural details of both countries, as well as the influences the West had on both, there is very little commentary on the difficulties there must have been in traveling between the two during a time of war. I find it hard to believe that the narrator and his father had such an easy time of coping with being in a country currently responsible for the slaughter of so many of their people, or that the Japanese themselves accepted them with minimal signs of anger and distrust. Again, I don't know the facts, so this is all personal perception of the matter.<br /><br />Lastly, I had an issue with the prose. Admittedly, I am biased against short sentences, but there were too many times when I was thrown off by the basic mechanics of word order and usage, and had to backtrack in order to figure out what the author was trying to say. Many of the better ones were beautiful, to be sure, but I feel that the novel could have used to a better editor. Also, the ending explanation of the epistolary form used throughout the novel was a weak one, and rather than tying up the conclusion left me with a deep feeling of disbelief.<br /><br />Ultimately, it was a very relaxing and casual novel that fed my craving for lands I have never been to, but didn't do much when it came to my craving for good literature.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4161560946049912803" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5364077-connie-g?shelf=asia">asia</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5364077-connie-g?shelf=japan">japan</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer374491028" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18393536867815314131">"The Samurai's Garden" is a touching, gentle novel filled with Japanese culture. A Chinese young man, Stephen, was sent to his family's vacation home in a coastal town in Japan to recuperate from tuberculosis. The artistic student spends time with the caretaker, Matsu, who has created serene gardens. Matsu is quiet, but has hidden depths of wisdom. Matsu has been taking care of an older woman, Sachi, in a leper colony since they were both teenagers. Although Stephen is the narrator of the story,</span>
  <span id="freeText18393536867815314131" style="display:none">"The Samurai's Garden" is a touching, gentle novel filled with Japanese culture. A Chinese young man, Stephen, was sent to his family's vacation home in a coastal town in Japan to recuperate from tuberculosis. The artistic student spends time with the caretaker, Matsu, who has created serene gardens. Matsu is quiet, but has hidden depths of wisdom. Matsu has been taking care of an older woman, Sachi, in a leper colony since they were both teenagers. Although Stephen is the narrator of the story, the lives of Matsu and Sachi were the most important focus of the book. Their devotion to each other under challenging circumstances taught Stephen the true meaning of love.<br /><br />The novel is set in 1937-38 when Japan invaded China. Occasional radio reports and letters tell of the Japanese advance. Stephen meets his first love, a Japanese girl, but her father has a strong anti-Chinese attitude.<br /><br />The story has elements of isolation and loneliness because Stephen was dealing with tuberculosis, and Sachi and the other lepers had to live away from society. It also had themes of loyalty, honor, and the quiet comfort of gardens. I enjoyed this lovely story, and wished I was reading it in Matsu's peaceful Japanese garden.</span>
  <a data-text-id="18393536867815314131" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer8606154672540130661">This was an excellent and peaceful book...it is about a young chinese man who is sent to his parent's summer home at the beach in Japan to recover from an illness. The caretaker of the home becomes his life-teacher and as the book unfolds he learns about relationships, how to find peace within himself, and about love and loss. It is written as excerpts from his journal and so it is from his perspective...as though we, the reader, are sharing his inner-most thoughts and feelings. It gave me an in</span>
  <span id="freeText8606154672540130661" style="display:none">This was an excellent and peaceful book...it is about a young chinese man who is sent to his parent's summer home at the beach in Japan to recover from an illness. The caretaker of the home becomes his life-teacher and as the book unfolds he learns about relationships, how to find peace within himself, and about love and loss. It is written as excerpts from his journal and so it is from his perspective...as though we, the reader, are sharing his inner-most thoughts and feelings. It gave me an insight into the beauty of Japan and the way of life in the remote parts of that country in the 1930s. I also particularly enjoyed it because the caretaker's garden is his reverent and therapeutic domain..something i can relate to. I had a sense of peace while reading this book and could not wait to find time to sit and read it in my free time. I would strongly recommend this book..it is an easy book to read and a quick read..one that I hated to see come to an end.</span>

<div id="review_893162533" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/893162533" />
    <a title="Diane Lynn" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9796451-diane-lynn"><img alt="Diane Lynn" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1513083871p2/9796451.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/893162533?book_show_action=true">Mar 27, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Diane Lynn" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Diane Lynn" href="/user/show/9796451-diane-lynn">Diane Lynn</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Diane Lynn by: </span>
      Jeannette
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9796451-diane-lynn?shelf=japan">japan</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9796451-diane-lynn?shelf=cultural">cultural</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9796451-diane-lynn?shelf=leprosy">leprosy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9796451-diane-lynn?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer893162533" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2357918026262844241"><b>Buddy read with Jeannette</b><br/><br/>On the face of it, The Samurai’s Garden is a beautifully told story of one young man’s journey to figure out who he is, a sort of coming of age story. But there is really so much more below the surface. There is an enchanting and poignant love story. The reader is shown how beauty can exist in a cruel world. There is betrayal, adultery, young love, and two gardens that play a large role as a place of peace. Plus other topics I don’t want to mention so as not to spoil any</span>
  <span id="freeText2357918026262844241" style="display:none"><b>Buddy read with Jeannette</b><br /><br />On the face of it, The Samurai’s Garden is a beautifully told story of one young man’s journey to figure out who he is, a sort of coming of age story. But there is really so much more below the surface. There is an enchanting and poignant love story. The reader is shown how beauty can exist in a cruel world. There is betrayal, adultery, young love, and two gardens that play a large role as a place of peace. Plus other topics I don’t want to mention so as not to spoil anything. All of this is framed by the Japanese invasion of China during 1937-1938. Writing this I wonder how it all came together to make a cohesive story, but it does, and with beautiful imagery. <br /><br />Stephen Chan is a young man of 20, recovering from tuberculosis. He is sent from his family’s home in Hong Kong to their summer beach home in Tarumi, Japan. Stephen is met at the Tarumi train station by Matsu, the family’s long time gardener/caretaker of the summer home. From the beginning it is clear that Matsu will not baby Stephen and that is just what Stephen needs at this point in his life. At first it seemed to me that Matsu was more the teacher or guide to Stephen. But really Matsu and Stephen form a bond and learn about life and family from each other. Stephen is introduced to other inhabitants of Tarumi who play a large role in his year long stay. Kenzo, Keiko and the most important, Sachi (an older woman who actually lives in Yamaguchi) all develop a relationship with Stephen. <br /><br />This story is told by way of entries in Stephen’s journal as he learns about loyalty to friends and family. Japanese culture is incorporated into the story. Read the book it really is wonderful!</span>
  <a data-text-id="2357918026262844241" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_301538806" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/301538806" />
    <a title="Karla" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2428829-karla"><img alt="Karla" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1544016408p2/2428829.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/301538806?book_show_action=true">Mar 27, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Karla" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Karla" href="/user/show/2428829-karla">Karla</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2428829-karla?shelf=friendship">friendship</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2428829-karla?shelf=2012">2012</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2428829-karla?shelf=historical">historical</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2428829-karla?shelf=japan">japan</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2428829-karla?shelf=china">china</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2428829-karla?shelf=mourning">mourning</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer301538806" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1641289033033536407">A gracefully illuminating novel that is tender and as delicate as a first blooming blossom in Spring. There is so much to discuss in this story, perfect for book clubs. At first it appears so simple, a story of a young Chinese-Japanese young man named Stephen leaving his family house in Hong Kong so his younger sister may not get exposed his sickness. So he travels to stay at his family's Summer shore side home in Tarumi, Japan to recoup from a battle with tuberculosis. He stays with Matsu a mas</span>
  <span id="freeText1641289033033536407" style="display:none">A gracefully illuminating novel that is tender and as delicate as a first blooming blossom in Spring. There is so much to discuss in this story, perfect for book clubs. At first it appears so simple, a story of a young Chinese-Japanese young man named Stephen leaving his family house in Hong Kong so his younger sister may not get exposed his sickness. So he travels to stay at his family's Summer shore side home in Tarumi, Japan to recoup from a battle with tuberculosis. He stays with Matsu a master gardener and the home's caretaker. As soon as I encountered Matsu a quite man with little to say and so much inner goodness I wished to be Stephen recovering and learning so much from a humble mentor with so much integrity it brings tears to my eyes. Matsu slowly unveils and shares his life, customs, friends and secrets to Stephen and embraces Stephen as one of his own. <br />I realized there were so many lessons and metaphors I adored about this writing like nature, time, aging, health, balance, love, humanity and so much more. I could study and tear apart this book in sections but I just simply enjoyed it. A powerfully moving comfort read to encounter once again one day as I mature and find myself needing enlightened once again. </span>
  <a data-text-id="1641289033033536407" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer135441681" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15867816700550132225">Bad, sentimental, insipid book about a young Chinese man from a wealthy Hong Kong family who is sent to a small village in Japan (why?) to convalesce from TB (?), only to discover a leper colony (named Yamaguchi lol) up in the mountains. The Japanese are conquering China, but the young man Stephen who loves to call himself Stephen-san (which he shouldn't do when speaking about himself) doesn't really care either for his country, his friends, or his family. Neither does he care about his illness,</span>
  <span id="freeText15867816700550132225" style="display:none">Bad, sentimental, insipid book about a young Chinese man from a wealthy Hong Kong family who is sent to a small village in Japan (why?) to convalesce from TB (?), only to discover a leper colony (named Yamaguchi lol) up in the mountains. The Japanese are conquering China, but the young man Stephen who loves to call himself Stephen-san (which he shouldn't do when speaking about himself) doesn't really care either for his country, his friends, or his family. Neither does he care about his illness, swimming in the sea, visiting people whose health has already been weakened by their disease, or kissing Japanese girls, all the time more or less happily spraying his surroundings with bacteria.<br /><br />What else? There is a supposedly taciturn and secretive gardener Matsu, who after a few days spills all his sweet red beans to Stephen-san. There is his lover Sachi who lives in the colony and is ashamed at not having committed seppuku long time ago (despite the fact that women didn't commit seppuku). There are a few other people, who very often say "I am honored to see you here" and not much more. There is a lot of Japanese, which is funny, because a lot of it is incorrect (<i>genken</i> instead of <i>genkan</i>, <i>yakata</i> instead of <i>yukata</i>, stuff like that). The Japanese people in general are completely dead, killed by their politeness, mysteriousness and I dunno? honor? and rice crackers.<br /><br />As long as books like these are being published, there is hope for every and all Orientalist charlatans under the sun. Cheers!</span>
  <a data-text-id="15867816700550132225" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2264195792" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tina " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tina " href="/user/show/14260681-tina">Tina </a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2264195792" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9648046640376031056">The Samurai’s Garden is so quiet and gentle in how it unfolds that it is hard to describe. Slow and somehow not dull, but beautiful. It’s about a friendship formed in a oceanside village in Japan between a Chinese student, a Japanese caretaker, and a beautiful Japanese woman disfigured by leprosy during 1937 and 1938 when their countries were at war. About finding beauty in the least likely of people and places. <br/><br/>“There are rare people who will help you carry your burdens through this life”. </span>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2594691948?book_show_action=true">Nov 30, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="ShirleyS" class="user" itemprop="url" name="ShirleyS" href="/user/show/80868575-shirleys">ShirleyS</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/80868575-shirleys?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2594691948" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3003514633749120581">Lovely book. I didn’t want it to end. I wanted to accompany Stephen back to Hong Kong to rejoin his mother and sister. Also, I wanted him to continue to have Matsu and his garden and his words of wisdom in his life. Sigh. The book ended too soon.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_315221738" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/315221738" />
    <a title="Patricia" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7408227-patricia"><img alt="Patricia" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1330544319p2/7408227.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/315221738?book_show_action=true">Apr 19, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Patricia" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Patricia" href="/user/show/7408227-patricia">Patricia</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer315221738" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16724866044615932401">A very old-fashioned book - sweet, slow, peaceful, with a gossamer-light style that will not appeal to all. It is nearly a fairy tale, and a very gentle one, at that. While it takes place during WWII (during Japan's massacre of Nanking), very little of the war intrudes. This is the author's choice, to show an interlude of the sort that took place for those stricken with TB (rather like the middle class Americans who would retreat to the Catskills in the late 19th C., early 20th C.). This is not </span>
  <span id="freeText16724866044615932401" style="display:none">A very old-fashioned book - sweet, slow, peaceful, with a gossamer-light style that will not appeal to all. It is nearly a fairy tale, and a very gentle one, at that. While it takes place during WWII (during Japan's massacre of Nanking), very little of the war intrudes. This is the author's choice, to show an interlude of the sort that took place for those stricken with TB (rather like the middle class Americans who would retreat to the Catskills in the late 19th C., early 20th C.). This is not an earth-shaking book, but one that comes across as an almost dreamlike remembrance. Flaws? You may be annoyed that there is little made of the massacre, of the war. You may be annoyed by Stephen's detachment from his real family and replacement with this one that is fairy tale-fantasy. There is a lot of mis-spelling/mis-use of Japanese words, which will annoy those with great familiarity with Japan. The protagonist somehow does not have a "male literary voice", despite being a 20 year old heterosexual male. For me, the latter was the most discordant note. The other male characters were fine, the phrasing Stephen used somehow felt overly feminine. Maybe too much politeness, carefulness in Stephen? I enjoyed the book largely for the fairy tale flavor and its slow, gentle pace. The book itself is like a zen garden.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16724866044615932401" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer7254827629332260721">Have you ever turned off the sound on a video or TV program, and just watched the picture? You see things you don’t see when you are distracted by the “noise.” That’s how this book felt for me--quiet and calm enough to expose some details I might otherwise have missed.   <span id="freeText7254827629332260721" style="display:none">Have you ever turned off the sound on a video or TV program, and just watched the picture? You see things you don’t see when you are distracted by the “noise.” That’s how this book felt for me--quiet and calm enough to expose some details I might otherwise have missed. And they were delicately beautiful details.<br /><br />One of my takeaways: creating order and carefully-tended beauty can help us deal with the ugly and uncontrollable things that exist in our lives.<br /><br />I love this kind of quiet and sensitive writing. While there were times when I thought Tsukiyama had taken it a bit too far, that the quietness here was possibly too much of a good thing, I was very much affected by the story. The distinctive characters and atmosphere will stay with me for a long time. <br /><br /><i>“He showed me that life is not just from within, it extends all around you, whether you wish it to or not.”</i></span>

<div id="review_584426247" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/584426247" />
    <a title="Mikayla" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9737674-mikayla"><img alt="Mikayla" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1337827422p2/9737674.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/584426247?book_show_action=true">Apr 07, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mikayla" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mikayla" href="/user/show/9737674-mikayla">Mikayla</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9737674-mikayla?shelf=shit-books">shit-books</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer584426247" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11662363769829482788">So, for starters, this book sucks. Not only is it vapid, shallow, and cliche, it is also factually incorrect. I'm just going to glaze over my problems with this book, or else I'd be writing a novel. First off, the way Tsukiyama wrote the Japanese parts bugged me. At one point Kieko, Stephen's love interest, is apologizing for how rudely her father acted towards Stephen. But instead of referring to her father as "Chichi", she refers to him as "otousan", which refers to someone else's father, whic</span>
  <span id="freeText11662363769829482788" style="display:none">So, for starters, this book sucks. Not only is it vapid, shallow, and cliche, it is also factually incorrect. I'm just going to glaze over my problems with this book, or else I'd be writing a novel. First off, the way Tsukiyama wrote the Japanese parts bugged me. At one point Kieko, Stephen's love interest, is apologizing for how rudely her father acted towards Stephen. But instead of referring to her father as "Chichi", she refers to him as "otousan", which refers to someone else's father, which is incorrect. That may seem a little nit-picky, but since I am in first year Japanese, and one of the first things we learn is how to differentiate the two. Also, another nitpick, how is it that Stephen can understand the Japanese citizens perfectly? At the beginning of the book we learn that Stephen only speaks a little Japanese. So does everyone in Tarumi and Yamaguchi conveniently know Chinese? English? Is someone translating for Stephen? I can understand why Matsu could know Chinese since he grew up around Stephen's father's family, but the rest of the village? And it doesn't help that Tsukiyama adds in the Japanese words in the middle of the conversation, further making it seem like they are speaking another language. And the conversations that the characters have are very complex for people who can supposedly only speak a few words in the others language. Secondly, why is Stephen kissing people if he is recuperating from TB? It's a highly contagious disease and in that time, there was no cure for it. Lastly, why is Stephen even in Japan in the first place? His family is letting him go live in a country that they're at war with, pretty much acting like it's no big deal. Why would the Japanese government be okay with this? And the family comes of as being very rich. Why wouldn't they have a country home somewhere safer? Wouldn't you think that letting your sick son go live in another country to recuperate is a bad idea. Overall, I hated it</span>
  <a data-text-id="11662363769829482788" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Smitha Murthy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3547543-smitha-murthy"><img alt="Smitha Murthy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1585489620p2/3547543.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2402925542?book_show_action=true">May 29, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Smitha Murthy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Smitha Murthy" href="/user/show/3547543-smitha-murthy">Smitha Murthy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/12988234-the-samurai-s-garden">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Fans of The Guest Cat. :-)</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3547543-smitha-murthy?shelf=contemporary">contemporary</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3547543-smitha-murthy?shelf=asia">asia</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3547543-smitha-murthy?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3547543-smitha-murthy?shelf=friendship">friendship</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3547543-smitha-murthy?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3547543-smitha-murthy?shelf=women-writers">women-writers</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2402925542" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12601880592248702593">How did I read 800+ books without discovering Gail Tsukiyama? What an utterly magical author! A couple of years ago, I read 'The Guest Cat', which a friend had recommended, and I didn't like that book at all. Even now, it's a joke between us. But the friend had suggested that book to me thinking that I would like the quiet, meditative quality in that book. She knows me too well - I do love 'quiet, meditative' books that ripple with stillness and poetry. But while 'The Guest Cat' didn't blink its</span>
  <span id="freeText12601880592248702593" style="display:none">How did I read 800+ books without discovering Gail Tsukiyama? What an utterly magical author! A couple of years ago, I read 'The Guest Cat', which a friend had recommended, and I didn't like that book at all. Even now, it's a joke between us. But the friend had suggested that book to me thinking that I would like the quiet, meditative quality in that book. She knows me too well - I do love 'quiet, meditative' books that ripple with stillness and poetry. But while 'The Guest Cat' didn't blink its whiskers at me, I fell utterly, magically, completely in love with 'The Samurai's Garden.'<br /><br />Set in Japan at about the beginning of the second World War, the book is a beautiful and evocative tribute to the unexpected friendships and relationships we make in life. When Stephen-san from Hong Kong is forced to stay in his grandfather's beach house retreat in the fishing village of Tarumi after a bout of sickness, it marks the beginning of a transformation in his life. He forms a strong bond with the caretaker, Matsu, and his soulmate, Sachi, who is banished from Tarumi. Old relationships are unfurled through Stephen's eyes - he learns of the fragile state of his parents' marriage, the complexity of relations in Tarumi, and feels the first spring of young love with Keiko. Against the backdrop of it all is the horror of war as the Japanese invade China.<br /><br /><br />So beautifully poetic and Zen-like, this is one of my favorite books of this year. Nay, ever.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12601880592248702593" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1534607417" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12503711477703993575">What is it about Japanese books that soothe the soul so well? The clean, bare rooms with tatami mats, kneeling for tea, the simple meals of rice and pickled vegetables, the good, pure joy of things being simple and beautiful in a quiet and understated way. I can't get enough of this kind of book. And these things are physical representations of the writing itself which is clean, simple, elegant, and real. I'm not finished reading the book yet, but it is completely engaging as it allows me to sli</span>
  <span id="freeText12503711477703993575" style="display:none">What is it about Japanese books that soothe the soul so well? The clean, bare rooms with tatami mats, kneeling for tea, the simple meals of rice and pickled vegetables, the good, pure joy of things being simple and beautiful in a quiet and understated way. I can't get enough of this kind of book. And these things are physical representations of the writing itself which is clean, simple, elegant, and real. I'm not finished reading the book yet, but it is completely engaging as it allows me to slip away from my world of clutter and bright colours and stress and spend some time in a Japanese garden contemplating the beauty of life and people. <br /><br />Set in the 1930s during the Japanese attach on China, this book nevertheless feels timeless. As its protagonist, Stephen, heals his body, I join him in his retreat, healing my mind. This is also one of the first Japanese books I have read that is set near the ocean, and that lends something even more relaxing and healing to the book. A retreat within Japan, which is itself a retreat from the consumerism and superficiality of the Western world. I think this book is like a haiku. It stills my soul and brings me peace.<br /><br />***<br /><br />I finished it now, and found this video which I would recommend to anyone who was wondering exactly what Japan looked like in the 1930s. <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=A4fkX1SVT0A">https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=A4fkX...</a> </span>
  <a data-text-id="12503711477703993575" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer14482772722499021721">I had never read a Tsukiyama book before, but was eager to, as I've heard such good things about her work. She seems to be a little neglected, unfortunately and undeservedly. Whilst perusing Goodreads reviews, I plumped for <i>The Samurai's Garden</i> as my introduction to her work, as I loved the sound of the plot. <br/><br/>I was incredibly satisfied with this novel. There are so many themes which are addressed here - illness, family, society, secrecy, difference, loneliness - all of which were handled with th</span>
  <span id="freeText14482772722499021721" style="display:none">I had never read a Tsukiyama book before, but was eager to, as I've heard such good things about her work. She seems to be a little neglected, unfortunately and undeservedly. Whilst perusing Goodreads reviews, I plumped for <i>The Samurai's Garden</i> as my introduction to her work, as I loved the sound of the plot. <br /><br />I was incredibly satisfied with this novel. There are so many themes which are addressed here - illness, family, society, secrecy, difference, loneliness - all of which were handled with the utmost dexterity. <i>The Samurai's Garden</i> is a beautifully written, rather quiet, and contemplative novel, and I very much look forward to my next Tsukiyama on its strength.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer13437028186347312485">Such a quiet, slow paced book, but yet so much happens. A person's life can change so much in one short year.<br/><br/>During World War II, a young Chinese man stays at his family's beach house in Japan. The war between Japan and China is always in the background of the book, never quite forgotten even though it does not seem to impact the lives of the main characters directly. During the course of the year, there are floods and fires, celebrations and funerals, failed relationships and happy reunions.<br/><br/>As </span>
  <span id="freeText13437028186347312485" style="display:none">Such a quiet, slow paced book, but yet so much happens. A person's life can change so much in one short year.<br /><br />During World War II, a young Chinese man stays at his family's beach house in Japan. The war between Japan and China is always in the background of the book, never quite forgotten even though it does not seem to impact the lives of the main characters directly. During the course of the year, there are floods and fires, celebrations and funerals, failed relationships and happy reunions.<br /><br />As autumn once again becomes autumn, everyone has changed, everyone has grown in some small but significant way.<br /><br />Two of the main characters, Matsu the caretaker and his friend, Sachi, have gardens. One has a garden of flowers, the other a garden of rocks. Both are beautiful and both represent their lives.<br /><br />Beautifully written, this is a story to remember after the last page has been turned over. The book reminds us that, no matter what, we are alive. Where there is life, there is hope.</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1557895518" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14307709673154825180">A peaceful and very pleasant read, dealing with leprosy and with nice characters.<br/><br/>Because he has tuberculosis, Stephen is sent to a holiday in Japan. He has to leave Hong Kong and his family and adapt to a life with Matsu, the silent housekeeper. While worring about the war between Japan and China, he discovers that some people in the village caught leprosy.<br/><br/>I really enjoyed the peaceful atmosphere. As the book was set in the 1930s, there wasn't really technology or speed, letters took weeks or mo</span>
  <span id="freeText14307709673154825180" style="display:none">A peaceful and very pleasant read, dealing with leprosy and with nice characters.<br /><br />Because he has tuberculosis, Stephen is sent to a holiday in Japan. He has to leave Hong Kong and his family and adapt to a life with Matsu, the silent housekeeper. While worring about the war between Japan and China, he discovers that some people in the village caught leprosy.<br /><br />I really enjoyed the peaceful atmosphere. As the book was set in the 1930s, there wasn't really technology or speed, letters took weeks or months to come, and hobbies were gardening, reading, listening music, painting, etc. The Japanese setting was nicely presented, with foods or customs in the relationships. It was entertaiting to see how Stephen, a modern 20 years old Chinese man, dealt with all of this and dicovered a new environment and new people. I really liked the leprosy side, it added a lot of depth and was also interesting because I knew few things about the illness. All the characters were very fine for me, with a lot of gentleness, the book maybe just lacked more dialogues (at the same time, it added to the quiet). The various romances dealt with interesting things, but they seemed more frustrating than anything. Overall, a well done book, conveying perfectly the old fashioned and asian atmosphere and a warm read about life, illness, death and love.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14307709673154825180" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1615879007" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1615879007" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9152134954524198889">The serenity and beauty of a Japanese garden and assistance of a samurai come through this story. Both aspects unveil a poignant story.<br/><br/>1937: Stephen Chan, 20 years old, is sent by his family from busy Hong Kong to his family’s summer house by the sea in Tarumi, Japan, to recover from tuberculosis. <br/><br/>He is met there by the only occupant of the house Matsu, who’s been taking care of the house and garden since early age. The garden reflects the extension of his life. In the village he is known for hi</span>
  <span id="freeText9152134954524198889" style="display:none">The serenity and beauty of a Japanese garden and assistance of a samurai come through this story. Both aspects unveil a poignant story.<br /><br />1937: Stephen Chan, 20 years old, is sent by his family from busy Hong Kong to his family’s summer house by the sea in Tarumi, Japan, to recover from tuberculosis. <br /><br />He is met there by the only occupant of the house Matsu, who’s been taking care of the house and garden since early age. The garden reflects the extension of his life. In the village he is known for his art as a master of gardens. He uses flowers as colors to paint his picture in the garden.<br /><br />While Stephen recuperates, he occupies his time with painting and drawing.<br /><br />At the beach, he meets two sisters Keiko and Mika. As he gets to know Keiko, he learns of her brother being with the Japanese army in China. That makes him wander if the brother is in Shanghai, where Japanese recently celebrated their victory over controlling the city.<br /><br />Matsu takes Stephen to Yamaguchi to visit his leper friend Sachi. This village in the mountains was designated just for lepers. With each visit Stephen gets to know Sachi more and he is fascinated by her calmness. He persists in convincing her to tell him her story. And it is a profound story.<br /><br />As the story develops, it reveals in bits the sad history of Japanese invasions, including unnecessary brutality. But the story is concentrated on human nature and what it takes to achieve calmness as human nature tends to struggle.<br /><br />The story has a bit slow beginning, but once the leper village is introduced (pg 23) the story picks up its pace and is touchingly presented.<br /><br />The book mentions women divers, if you’d like to read more about this: The Island of Sea Women by Lisa See – about Korean island of Jeju known for women divers. <br /><br />Highly recommend another book about the issue of lepers: The Island by Victoria Hislop<br /><br />@FB/BestHistoricalFiction</span>
  <a data-text-id="9152134954524198889" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        rated it

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780641961557" href="/book/show/6979827-the-samurai-s-garden">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer670881037" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6073645828899787594"><a href='http://bookwormscloset.com/the-samurais-garden/' rel='nofollow noopener'>
  <img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1500578682i/23361108._SY540_.jpg' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/>
</a><br/><br/><b>
  <i>It’s harder than I imagined, to be alone. I suppose I might get used to it, like an empty canvas you slowly begin to fill.</i>
</b><br/><br/>Stephen, a young Chinese man with Tuberculosis travels alone to the family cottage in Japan during WWII. Here he lives a quiet life far from war, family and friends. The only company he has is the silent Matsu who looks after the house and garden. Slowly a beautiful friendship emerges between the two men.<br/><br/><b>
  <i>Matsu scared away most people with his aloofness, but I was s</i></b></span>
  <span id="freeText6073645828899787594" style="display:none"><a href="http://bookwormscloset.com/the-samurais-garden/" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1500578682i/23361108._SY540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><br><b>
  <i>It’s harder than I imagined, to be alone. I suppose I might get used to it, like an empty canvas you slowly begin to fill.</i>
</b><br><br>Stephen, a young Chinese man with Tuberculosis travels alone to the family cottage in Japan during WWII. Here he lives a quiet life far from war, family and friends. The only company he has is the silent Matsu who looks after the house and garden. Slowly a beautiful friendship emerges between the two men.<br><br><b>
  <i>Matsu scared away most people with his aloofness, but I was something more. He seemed to have a story no one had bothered to discover.</i>
</b><br><br>Matsu introduces Stephen to a small community of leprosy and the beautiful Sachi who has lost part of her face. She hides from the outside world, and rarely comes out of her house and her stone garden. Slowly she opens up to Stephen and we hear her fascinating story.<br><br><b>
  <i>There’s something more serious about the fall than any other season. Maybe it’s the light that gradually grows darker, making everything seem less trivial, forcing you to look harder to find your way.</i>
</b><br><br>'The Samurai's Garden' is one of the most beautiful books I have read in a long time. It is a deep, thoughtful, sad and beautiful tale of friendship, love and beauty.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6073645828899787594" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2718203051" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2718203051" />
    <a title="Scott" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6202132-scott"><img alt="Scott" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1531237754p2/6202132.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2718203051?book_show_action=true">Feb 25, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Scott" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Scott" href="/user/show/6202132-scott">Scott</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2718203051" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10571695710114627193">Monotonous prose. Every character sounds like every other character. Stilted, stereotyped, and preciously simple - presumably to evoke the ethereal experience of life in “The Samurai’s Garden.” (WTF do samurai have to do with this story?) Apart from an almost throwaway revelation near the end, this novel is as shallow and unmemorable as an article in an in-flight magazine. Thank God the word “Zen” does not appear in the text. It really reads as if a nice California lady read a couple of Wikipedi</span>
  <span id="freeText10571695710114627193" style="display:none">Monotonous prose. Every character sounds like every other character. Stilted, stereotyped, and preciously simple - presumably to evoke the ethereal experience of life in “The Samurai’s Garden.” (WTF do samurai have to do with this story?) Apart from an almost throwaway revelation near the end, this novel is as shallow and unmemorable as an article in an in-flight magazine. Thank God the word “Zen” does not appear in the text. It really reads as if a nice California lady read a couple of Wikipedia articles about Japan (and Japanese gardens!) and decided to write a book.<br /><br />The seemingly random littering of Japanese words throughout the book made me wonder - again - WTF the author is trying to accomplish? Special annoyance points for the consistent misspelling of “genkan” and the narrator’s referring to himself, at least once, as “Stephen-san.”<br /><br />Then there’s the girl who commits “seppuku” with her father’s fishing knife... Does the author think seppuku just means suicide? Or did the teenage girl ritually eviscerate herself like a samurai warrior?<br /><br />Also, would a Hong Kong Chinese family send their tubercular teenage son to stay in Japan, for his health, in the late 1930’s? AND this boy - without hesitation or comment - kisses and makes love to a Japanese girl (when his own beloved sister isn’t allowed to be in the same room with him, for her own safety)? What am I supposed to think?</span>
  <a data-text-id="10571695710114627193" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_28496040" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/28496040" />
    <a title="Jordan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1353878-jordan"><img alt="Jordan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1350584604p2/1353878.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/28496040?book_show_action=true">Jul 28, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jordan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jordan" href="/user/show/1353878-jordan">Jordan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer28496040" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11359686095925031049">While a good read I would have to say this is my least favourite Gail Tsukiyama novel to date. While a great story and a touching one like most of her other novels I somehow wanted this story to give me more and was expecting it do so only be a little let down in the end. I also found fault with the author's attempt to try and non-stigmatize leprosy only to add fuel to the stigma such as that lepers skin rot and have a smell of rotting. That is just plain not true as lepers skin does not in fact</span>
  <span id="freeText11359686095925031049" style="display:none">While a good read I would have to say this is my least favourite Gail Tsukiyama novel to date. While a great story and a touching one like most of her other novels I somehow wanted this story to give me more and was expecting it do so only be a little let down in the end. I also found fault with the author's attempt to try and non-stigmatize leprosy only to add fuel to the stigma such as that lepers skin rot and have a smell of rotting. That is just plain not true as lepers skin does not in fact 'rot' nor is their an odor from it. She also describes lepers with missing limbs and fingers as if it is common with leprosy but in fact most often then not it is only skin lesions caused by severe nerve damage and contrary to popular conception, leprosy does not actually cause body parts to simply fall off. I'll get off my soapbox now but again, if trying to destroy age old social stigmas please do a more thorough research into the matter as a novelist. Still Gail Ysukiyama is fabulous, remains one of my favourites, and any of her novels are well worth the read.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="11359686095925031049" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('fr3zdhnw9aRBBn3ZxvbEEJgQxVqd6z98ZyGp8Xl+m4tEm5qkoq48zOYtybgxA87PTk8iklNmsRs8X+9AtD1pEQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

After months in the fetid, overcrowded jail, she learns she is sentenced to “the land beyond the seas,” Van Diemen’s Land, a penal colony in Australia. Though uncertain of what awaits, Evangeline knows one thing: the child she carries will be born on the months-long voyage to this distant land.\n\nDuring the journey on a repurposed slave ship, the Medea, Evangeline strikes up a friendship with Hazel, a girl little older than her former pupils who was sentenced to seven years transport for stealing a silver spoon. Canny where Evangeline is guileless, Hazel -- a skilled midwife and herbalist – is soon offering home remedies to both prisoners and sailors in return for a variety of favors.\n\nThough Australia has been home to Aboriginal people for more than 50,000 years, the British government in the 1840s considers its fledgling colony uninhabited and unsettled, and views the natives as an unpleasant nuisance. By the time the Medea arrives, many of them have been forcibly relocated, their land seized by white colonists. One of these relocated people is Mathinna, the orphaned daughter of the Chief of the Lowreenne tribe, who has been adopted by the new governor of Van Diemen’s Land.\n\nIn this gorgeous novel, Christina Baker Kline brilliantly recreates the beginnings of a new society in a beautiful and challenging land, telling the story of Australia from a fresh perspective, through the experiences of Evangeline, Hazel, and Mathinna. While life in Australia is punishing and often brutally unfair, it is also, for some, an opportunity: for redemption, for a new way of life, for unimagined freedom. Told in exquisite detail and incisive prose, The Exiles is a story of grace born from hardship, the unbreakable bonds of female friendships, and the unfettering of legacy.<\/span>\n  <a       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/28719.Gail_Tsukiyama">About Gail Tsukiyama</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer4348747525022696580">Born to a Chinese mother and a Japanese father in San Francisco, Gail Tsukiyama now lives in El Cerrito, California. Her novels include <i>Women of the Silk</i> (1991), <i>The Samurai's Garden</i> (1995), <i>Night of Many Dreams</i> (1998), <i>The Language of Threads</i> (1999), <i>Dreaming Water</i> (2002), and <i>The Street of a Thousand Blossoms</i> (2007).</span>

</div>
